<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="02af3915068544b9b51273ea44178bbf"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="02af3915068544b9b51273ea44178bbf">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '21cd874f-c601-46e1-b896-0ad9fe37f721'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d109708553" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>The Fe-based anode of sodium-ion batteries attracts much attention due to the abundant source, low-cost, and high specific capacity. However, the low electron and ion transfer rate, poor structural stability, and shuttle effect of NaS<sub>2</sub> intermediate restrain its further development. Herein, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node spawned N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure (FHNCS) was designed using the modified MIL-88B(Fe) as a template followed by catalytic growth and sulfidation process. During catalytic growth process, the reduced Fe monomers catalyze the growth of N-doped carbon nanotubes to connect the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node, forming a 3D scaffold structure. Wherein the N-doped carbon promotes the transfer of electrons between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS particles, and the tri-heterojunction facilitates the diffusion of electrons at the interface, to organize a 3D conductive network. The unique scaffold structure provides more active sites and shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path. Meanwhile, the structure exhibits excellent mechanical stability to alleviate the volume expansion during circulation. Furthermore, the Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction can adjust the d-band center of Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to enhance the adsorption between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S intermediate, which restrains the shuttle effect. Therefore, the FHNCS demonstrates a high specific capacity of 436 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 84.7% and 73.4% of the initial capacities are maintained after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. We believe that this strategy gives an inspiration for constructing Fe-based anode with excellent rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="eem212684-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="eem212684-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been used in public lives as a form of energy storage and have provided great help to social development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0001_R_d109708465e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Rare Metals">1</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0003_R_d109708465e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Clean. Prod.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the limited resource and uneven distribution of lithium are not conducive to sustainable development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0004_R_d109708465e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Appl. Therm. Eng.">4</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0005_R_d109708465e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have the advantages of low cost and high safety, which are expected to replace LIBs in some fields.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0006_R_d109708465e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electro. Ener. Rev.">6</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0008_R_d109708465e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> is larger than Li<sup>+</sup>, resulting&nbsp;in severe volume expansion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0009_R_d109708465e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0010_R_d109708465e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile,&nbsp;the intrinsic electron conductivity of the electrode is poor, which leads to awful performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0011_R_d109708465e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The development of sodium storage materials with excellent rate capability and cycling stability, high energy, and power density is the key to achieving the practical application of SIBs, which can fill the vacancy in the energy storage field resulting from the high cost of LIBs. High expectations have been placed on transition metal oxides (sulfides) due to their high theoretical specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0012_R_d109708465e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0016_R_d109708465e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the Fe-based oxide/sulfide (FOS) exhibits many advantages of simple preparation, abundant resource, and environmental friendliness, which has become the focus of anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0017_R_d109708465e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, its poor electron and ion conductivity, the large volume change during the cycling process, and the shuttle effect of Na<sub>2</sub>S intermediate (generated from the discharge process of iron-based sulfur) limit their further development.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0018_R_d109708465e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Electrochim. Acta">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Constructing carbon coating FOS core-shell structure is a predominant approach to improve electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0019_R_d109708465e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Mater. Tech.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For one thing, the carbon coating can improve the electron conductivity between FOS particles, which decreases the interfacial transfer impedance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0020_R_d109708465e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">20</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0022_R_d109708465e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For another thing, the carbon layer can support the FOS particles to manufacture the core-shell structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0023_R_d109708465e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This unique structure provides more active sites for redox reaction and shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path to increase Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0024_R_d109708465e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Inter. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fast electron and ion diffusion promote reversible electrochemical performance, which is beneficial to rate capability and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0025_R_d109708465e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - CCS Chem.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Zhao constructed a kind of highly crystalline mesoporous Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>&nbsp;nanoparticles encapsulated in a hollow nitrogen-doped carbon nanosphere (HCM-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@void@N-C). The unique yolk-shell nanostructure can increase the contacting area between electrolyte and materials, the outer N-doped carbon shell can improve conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0026_R_d109708465e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thirdly, the outer carbon shell can protect the inner FOS particles, and the free space between the core and shell can relieve volume expansion during discharging process, achieving excellent mechanical stability, which improves the cycling stability of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0027_R_d109708465e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Alloys Comp.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the cycling stability and rate capability of FOS are improved by manufacturing FOS@C core-shell structure.</p>
         
         <p>Formation of heterojunction is doubtlessly another effective method to promote the electron transfer in the bulk of FOS heterojunction,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0028_R_d109708465e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> combining with fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic to foster fast reversible electrochemical reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0029_R_d109708465e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A new core-shell Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@FeS with a regular octahedral morphology was prepared by Xinglong Wu and his co-works using commercial Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a precursor. As an anode of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@FeS//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F full cell battery, a reversible specific capacity of 157 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved after 50 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 92.3% due to the fast electron transfer rate at the interface between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0030_R_d109708465e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Mater. Chem. Front.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially, the directed transfer of electron through the tri-heterojunction interface, such as SnS<sub>2</sub>/Mn<sub>2</sub>SnS<sub>4</sub>/Carbon tri-heterostructure, can facilitate the diffusion of electrons for high rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0031_R_d109708465e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0032_R_d109708465e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Nano">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, in the heterojunction system, the d-band center of the Fe element in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can be adjusted to increase the adsorption with Na<sub>2</sub>S intermediate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0033_R_d109708465e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combined with the physical confinement of the core-shell structure, the shuttle effect can be restrained. Therefore, designing the core-shell structure of FOS@C heterojunction is a delicate way to promote electron and ion diffusion kinetic, relieve volume expansion, and restrain the shuttle effect, which leads to excellent rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we designed a Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node spawned N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure (FHNCS) using the modified MIL-88B as a template followed by catalytic growth and sulfidation process. During the catalytic growth process, the reduced Fe monomers catalyze the growth of N-doped carbon nanotubes to connect the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node, forming a 3D scaffold structure. Wherein the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction is the core, and the N-doped carbon nanotube is the shell. Each component plays its role. The N-doped carbon promotes the transfer of electrons between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS particles, and the tri-heterojunction facilitates the diffusion of electrons at the interface, to organize a 3D conductive network from outer to inner. The unique scaffold structure provides a multi-channel for the permeation of electrolytes, which offers more active sites and shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path. Meanwhile, inspired by the scaffold, the structure exhibits excellent mechanical stability to effectively alleviate the volume expansion during circulation. Furthermore, the Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction can adjust the d-band center of Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to enhance the interaction between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S intermediate, which restrains the shuttle effect. Therefore, as the anode of SIBs, the FHNCS demonstrates excellent rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="eem212684-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="eem212684-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The preparation procedure of FHNCS contains the synthesis of a modified MIL-88B(Fe) template, catalytic growth process, and sulfidation stage. Firstly, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0001">1</a>, the lamellar stacked MIL-88B(Fe) template was prepared using citric acid as a modifying agent, which was further testified by the SEM in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2a</a>. Secondly, taking the Fe as the catalyst, the scaffold structure was constructed during the catalytic growth process. At this initial stage, the lamellar-stacked MIL-88B(Fe) was gradually broken to form Fe-based particles embedded in carbon nanosheet, and due to the reduction of hydrogen, the elemental Fe was generated. After that, taking the Fe as the catalytic center, the N-doped carbon nanotubes started to grow and connect crossly the Fe-based particles. However, when the temperature was lower than 600 °C, the Fe-based particles aggregated and grew only, and no nanotube was detected until 700 °C as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information. The Fe-based particles spawned the N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure was constructed at a temperature of 750 °C for 2 h as shown in Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2b</a>. Thirdly, the FHNCS was achieved after the final sulfidation process, wherein the content of N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure was calculated to be 38.47 wt% based on the TGA result as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4027268-2f68-4e90-a4a8-37fe5264d962/eem212684-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4027268-2f68-4e90-a4a8-37fe5264d962/eem212684-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/869baf83-ce06-4d5a-b95d-07dfaf086b12/eem212684-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4027268-2f68-4e90-a4a8-37fe5264d962/eem212684-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the preparation process of FHNCS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5894ad8e-fc53-4e8e-87eb-6890d76a367e/eem212684-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5894ad8e-fc53-4e8e-87eb-6890d76a367e/eem212684-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52ef6db7-1b2f-4f47-a641-91bab49c2771/eem212684-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5894ad8e-fc53-4e8e-87eb-6890d76a367e/eem212684-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) The SEM images of lamellar stacked MIL-88B(Fe), Fe-based particles node spawned N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure and FHNCS. d) The TEM images and e) element mapping of FHNCS. f) The HRTEM images. g) The selected area electron diffraction of FHNCS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The SEM and TEM were performed to characterize the morphology of FHNCS. As shown in SEM of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2c</a>, an obvious scaffold structure taking the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction particles as intersection, the 3D cross-crossed carbon nanotubes as bridging was achieved. Meanwhile, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2d</a>, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS core and the coated hollow carbon shell in the scaffold structure were clarified clearly. The unique structure is beneficial to mechanical stability and more active sites for redox reactions. The element distribution was checked by the element mapping of TEM. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information, the Fe, S, and O elements were focused on the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS core which was luminous, but the C and N elements were distributed around the outer carbon shell. Furthermore, the HRTEM and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) both were applied to analyze the phase composition. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0002">2f</a>, although the lattice fringes were not clear due to the generation of the abundant defect along with the formation of tri-heterojunction, the optical opacity, and magnetism of the solid heterojunction particle, a lattice fringe of 0.330 nm attributed to (002) of graphite was detected.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0034_R_d109708465e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the tri-heterojunction resulting from the intersection between the (102) lattice of Fe, (102) lattice of FeS, and (311) lattice of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with obvious interfaces was checked.</p>
         
         <p>The phase, surface functional group, and electronic structure information in FHNCS were further illustrated by the spectral characterizations taking the FANCS, FCS, and FAP as comparisons. As shown in XRD of <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, the FHNCS and FANCS corresponded to three phases of Fe (PDF#03-1050), Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (PDF#88-0866), and FeS (PDF#24-0080), while no Fe was detected in the XRD curves of FCS and FAP. It proves that the addition of dicyandiamide during calcination will reduce the ferric oxide to Fe, the addition of citric acid has little effect on the phase but the morphology of the material as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information. Then, the FTIR was used to study the Fe–O and Fe–S bonds (Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3b</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0035_R_d109708465e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Mater. Lett.">35</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0036_R_d109708465e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the Raman spectroscopy was applied to investigate the carbonization degree. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3c</a>, the D and G bands can be divided into four peaks, wherein the ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G1</sub> represents the content of defect in the carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0037_R_d109708465e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0038_R_d109708465e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be seen that the FHNCS demonstrated the highest ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G1</sub>, meaning abundant defect in FHNCS due to the introduction of the N element, which promotes electron conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0039_R_d109708465e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Powder Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0040_R_d109708465e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We conducted the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements for checking the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3d</a>) and pore distribution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3e</a>). The FHNCS exhibited a larger BET specific surface area of 56.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and pore volume due to its unique scaffold structure, providing more active sites for redox reactions during the charging and discharging process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0041_R_d109708465e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the electronic structure of the elements in FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP was analyzed by the XPS test. In the high-resolution analysis of Fe2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3f</a>), the peaks corresponding to Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> were detected in all the samples, which was consistent with the valence state of Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0042_R_d109708465e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Hazard. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0043_R_d109708465e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - IEEE Trans. Magn.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with FAP and FCS (724.6 and 712.4 eV for Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p1/2 and Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p3/2), the lower binding energy signified the increase of the electron density of Fe due to fast electron transfer at the interface of tri-heterojunction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0044_R_d109708465e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Alloy Compd.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the Fe<sup>0</sup> was discovered at a binding energy of 707.3 eV in FHNCS and FANCS, combing with the morphology, it gave evidence the Fe<sup>0</sup> generated from the reduction of dicyandiamide could catalyze the growth of N-carbon nanotube to form scaffold structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0045_R_d109708465e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Mater. Sci. Engine B">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the Fe–S and S<sup>2−</sup> bonds in the high-resolution analysis of S2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3g</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0046_R_d109708465e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Lett.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the Fe–O bond in the high-resolution analysis of O2p were clarified (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3h</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0017_R_d109708465e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which further proved the phase composition. The presence or not of peaks corresponding to N1s position was related to dicyandiamide (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0003">3i</a>). Furthermore, the high ratio of the integral area corresponding to pyridine N and pyrrole N was detected in FHNCS, demonstrating an abundant defect in N-carbon nanotube,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0047_R_d109708465e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Phys. Chem. C">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which was beneficial to conductivity. Therefore, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS heterojunction encapsulated by the N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure was formed, benefiting to transfer of electrons and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d0622496-2992-4538-abed-f6ff0005a345/eem212684-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d0622496-2992-4538-abed-f6ff0005a345/eem212684-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15772afd-b633-4322-9b65-14a1b937bb6d/eem212684-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d0622496-2992-4538-abed-f6ff0005a345/eem212684-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–e) The XRD patterns, FTIR curves, Raman spectrums, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms, and pore size distribution curves of FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP. f–i) The XPS high-resolution of Fe2p, S2p, O1s, and N1s of FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To explore the effect of morphology and phase on electrochemical performance, using FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP as cathodes, the half-cell was assembled. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed to study the electrochemical behavior. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4a</a>, the CV curves of FHNCS and FANCS at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a voltage window of 0.01–2.8 V demonstrated four pairs of distinct redox peaks of 0.52 and 1.05 V, 1.08 and 1.49 V, 1.48 and 1.63 V, and 1.84 and 2.21 V, which were attributed to the redox reactions of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, and Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0014_R_d109708465e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0017_R_d109708465e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> But only the peaks corresponding to redox reactions of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS were clarified in FCS and FAP. It gave side evidence for the difference in phases. Corresponding to the redox peaks of CV curves, as shown in Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4b</a>, the galvanostatic charge–discharge curve of FHNCS at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> exhibited four plateaus with higher specific capacity. The rate capability of materials was detected by the galvanostatic charge–discharge test at different current densities. As in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4c</a>, the FHNCS exhibited higher specific capacities of 540, 454, 394, 294, 236, and 191 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with obvious charging and discharging plateaus (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. A specific capacity of 485 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was returned when the current density was switched back to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which indicated excellent rate capability. Even when compared to the previously reported Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based materials, the FHNCS exhibited superior rate capability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0014_R_d109708465e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0020_R_d109708465e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">20</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0024_R_d109708465e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Inter. Sci.">24</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0048_R_d109708465e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0050_R_d109708465e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Small">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The distinguished rate capability was caused by the fast Na<sup>+</sup> and electron transfer rate due to the unique structure. The cycling stability was checked by the galvanostatic charge–discharge test. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4f</a>, an initial specific capacity of 466 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained, still 390 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained after 100 cycles. In contrast, FAP had a similar initial specific capacity with FHNCS, but decayed rapidly. The FANCS and FCS owned low specific capacity. Meanwhile, a specific capacity of 289 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained after a superlong 1000 cycles at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0004">4g</a>), and a specific capacity of 131.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained after 500 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), signifying excellent cycling stability resulting from the good mechanical stability of the scaffold structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9222d65e-8392-4bbb-9f26-b6a3b54cb622/eem212684-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9222d65e-8392-4bbb-9f26-b6a3b54cb622/eem212684-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b6575746-20ba-4ca6-b0dc-afcada028837/eem212684-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9222d65e-8392-4bbb-9f26-b6a3b54cb622/eem212684-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP. a) The CV curves at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a voltage window from 0.01 to 2.8 V. b) The galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the third cycle at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The cycling stability at different current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, 5.0, and 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of FHNCS at different current densities. e) A comparison of the rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0014" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0014_R_d109708465e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0020" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0020_R_d109708465e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">20</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0024" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0024_R_d109708465e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Inter. Sci.">24</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0048" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0048_R_d109708465e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0050" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0050_R_d109708465e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Small">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> f, g) The cycling stability at a current density of 0.5 and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Compared with other samples, the FHNCS demonstrated remarkable rate capability and cycling stability, which was discussed in detail as follows. Firstly, the open pores of hollow nanotubes provided channels for the permeation of electrolyte, which increased the contacting area with materials, offered more active sites for a redox reaction, and shortened the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths, resulting in fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic. The CV curves at different scan rates of 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, 4.0, and 5.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a–d</a>, Supporting Information) were performed, and according to the relation between current density and scan rate, the <i>b</i> value related to sodium storage behavior was calculated. As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5a</a>, the largest <i>b</i> value of the FHNCS indicated the highest pseudocapacitive contribution corresponding to surface control. Furthermore, the specific pseudocapacitive contribution was calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8e–h</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5b</a>, the pseudocapacitive contribution increased with the increase of the scan rate, and at the same scan rate of 3.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the FHNCS had the highest pseudocapacitive contribution of 69.86%, which derived the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport. To investigate the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup>, the&nbsp;&nbsp;GITT test was performed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The&nbsp;FHNCS exhibited the largest average diffusion coefficient as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5c</a>, which can be testified by the steep slope of the fitting line according to the low-frequency area of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5d</a>). Thus, the FHNCS demonstrated the lowest interfacial impedance signified by the semicircle at the low-frequency area of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Secondly, the 3D N-carbon nanotube provided a fast electron transfer bridge between the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction particles, and the formation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction promoted fast electron transfer in the bulk of the particle, wherein a 3D intersection from outer to the inner conductive network was achieved. Using the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, and Fe lattices as comparisons, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/Fe(102), FeS(102)/Fe(102), and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/FeS(102) heterojunctions were constructed as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5e</a>. The geometries of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, and Fe with different ways of exposing atoms were optimized, the most stable Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311), FeS(102), and Fe(102) were applied to fabricate the heterojunctions. The density of state (DOS) of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, the heterojunctions as Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/Fe(102), FeS(102)/Fe(102), and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/FeS(102) were calculated as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5f</a>. The DOS of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/Fe(102), FeS(102)/Fe(102), and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/FeS(102) heterojunctions exhibited the crossing Fermi level. Moreover, the DOS of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/Fe(102), FeS(102)/Fe(102), and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311)/FeS(102) is larger relative to Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS near the Fermi level, implying that heterojunctions had relatively large effective electron transfer number for facilitating reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0051_R_d109708465e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Energy Chem.">51</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0052_R_d109708465e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Ppl. Catal. B Environ.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This provided direct evidence that the electron can be transferred fleetly in the bulk of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction, companying with the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic, the electrochemical reactions of FHNCS were improved, which was proved by the slight change of peak current with the increase of scan rate as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2e522066-705a-41ff-92e6-daa420e6e454/eem212684-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2e522066-705a-41ff-92e6-daa420e6e454/eem212684-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b407bb23-eedb-4cf0-a751-6d9b550e7ddd/eem212684-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2e522066-705a-41ff-92e6-daa420e6e454/eem212684-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The CV curves of FHNCS at different scan rates. b) The logarithm peak current and scan rate plot to calculate <i>b</i> values of FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP. c) The average diffusion coefficients of FHNCS, FANCS, FCS, and FAP calculated according to the GITT test. d) The relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and the square root of frequency (ω<sup>−1/2</sup>) in the low-frequency region. e–g) Slab model, DOS calculation and Fe_d center energy of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(311), FeS(102), Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/FeS, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe, FeS/Fe heterojunctions according to DFT calculation.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The ex-situ XRD and XPS were recorded to prove the reversible electrochemical reaction of FHNCS. As shown in XRD curves with different voltages (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0006">6a</a>), at the initial state of the discharging process, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, and Fe were detected, and during discharging process, the relative intensity of diffraction peaks decreased as well as the increase of diffraction peaks corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>S. On the contrary, during the charging process, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS appeared gradually with a decrease of Na<sub>2</sub>S, signifying a reversible electrochemical reaction. Meanwhile, according to Fe2p, S2p, and O1s high-resolution of XPS analysis as shown in Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0006">6b–d</a>, during the discharging process, the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> was generated, and the peak intensity attributed to Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> resulted from reactions between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeS, and Na was increased along with the decrease of peak intensity corresponding to Fe–O bond. Furthermore, when charging, the peaks corresponding to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> decreased as well as increased of Fe–O bond gradually. Both the XRD and XPS results clarified the reversible electrochemical reactions. Thirdly, the d band energy level of Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can be regulated by forming Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0005">5g</a>, compared to pure Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the d band energy center of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction shifted to high energy, which was beneficial to bonding between Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and S in Na<sub><i>n</i></sub>S<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0053_R_d109708465e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem. Eng. J.">53</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0054_R_d109708465e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Batter. Supercaps">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the formation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction can increase the adsorption of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with Na<sub><i>n</i></sub>S<sub>2</sub> intermedium generated from the reaction between FeS and Na to restrain the shuttle effect, which improved cycling stability. Fourthly, the unique scaffold structure possessed excellent mechanical stability, which prevented aggregation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS particles and relieved the volume expansion during the discharging process. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0006">6e,f</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information, the scaffold structure was maintained perfectly after 50 cycles, even after 500 cycles, demonstrating excellent mechanical stability, which was beneficial to cycling stability. Therefore, as the anode of SIBs, the FHNCS exhibited a remarkable rate&nbsp;capability and cycling stability due to its advantages as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0007">7</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb657135-e468-4ba1-885a-3aabb70c1606/eem212684-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb657135-e468-4ba1-885a-3aabb70c1606/eem212684-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4bee0e62-ce66-4cc4-ad37-b9bc38a7f9c3/eem212684-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb657135-e468-4ba1-885a-3aabb70c1606/eem212684-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The XRD patterns. b–d) The XPS high-resolution of Fe2p, S2p and O1s in FHCNS. e, f) The SEM images of FHNCS after 50 and 500 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc942219-6cf0-4197-8f6b-b2c276a478aa/eem212684-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc942219-6cf0-4197-8f6b-b2c276a478aa/eem212684-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d1d2de6-795d-435a-8698-b0574bdca7c6/eem212684-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc942219-6cf0-4197-8f6b-b2c276a478aa/eem212684-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The advantages of FHNCS as the anode of SIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Finally, to detect the practical value of scaffold structure on electrochemical performance, taking the previously constructed NVP@C as cathode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0055_R_d109708465e1467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Chem. Res. Chinese U.">55</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0056_R_d109708465e1467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Rare Metals">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the FHNCS as an anode, the FHNCS//NVP@C full cell was assembled as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8a</a>. In Figure <a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8b</a>, the CV curve with a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> of NVP@C was measured to check the electrochemical behavior. A pair of redox peaks at 3.25 and 3.52 V was detected, correspondingly a pair of long platforms were found in the charging and discharging curves under the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8c</a>. Furthermore, the specific capacity of the FHNCS was 3.42 times than the specific capacity of NVP@C, thus, to balance the capacity between the positive and negative electrodes, the mass ratio of NVP@C/FHNCS was set as 3.42. The calculation formula is as follows:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="eem212684-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c7b1800d-3e21-47ae-8285-e4b2fa6a487f/eem212684-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:25750356:media:eem212684:eem212684-math-0001" display="block" overflow="scroll" location="graphic/eem212684-math-0001.png">
                     <mrow>
                        <mspace width="0.25em"></mspace>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>m</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                        </msub>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>C</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                        </msub>
                        <mo linebreak="goodbreak">=</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>m</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                        </msub>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>C</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                        </msub>
                     </mrow></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where the <i>m</i><sub>A</sub> and <i>m</i><sub>C</sub> are the mass of the anode and cathode, and the <i>C</i><sub>A</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>C</sub> are the specific capacities of the anode and cathode. The capacity was calculated based on the mass of anode. Firstly, the CV with voltage windows of 0.01–3.8 V at different scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> was performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8d</a>, two pairs of redox peaks at 1.89 and 1.95 V, and 2.15 and 2.91 V were tested, and with the increase of scan rate, the voltage of peak current was almost unchanged, implying the small&nbsp;polarization of the full cell, which benefited the reversible electrochemical reaction. Additionally, a pair of long and stable charging and discharging platforms in the charge–discharge curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8e</a>) with a high initial coulombic efficiency of 80.5% was&nbsp;achieved, which was beneficial to stable energy output. The rate capability was checked by the galvanostatic charge–discharge test. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8f</a>, the full cell demonstrated the high specific capacities of 160, 149, 144, 132, 126, 123, 104, and 70 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at different current densities of 0.02, 0.07, 0.1, 0.3, 0.4, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density was returned to 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a closed to initial specific capacity of 147 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an obvious charging and discharging platform was obtained, meaning excellent rate capability. Finally, the cycling stability of the full cell was characterized by the galvanostatic charge–discharge test at 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. As&nbsp;demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#eem212684-fig-0008">8h</a>, a high specific capacity of&nbsp;157 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was produced under the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, after 100 cycles, still a specific capacity of&nbsp;127 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained. A high initial specific capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained even at a high current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, signifying excellent cycling stability. In summary, both the Na//FHNCS half-cell and FHNCS//NVP@C full cell represented remarkable rate capability and cycling stability due to their fast Na<sup>+</sup> and electron diffusion rate, the strong adsorption with Na<sub>2</sub>S, and excellent mechanical stability.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="eem212684-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3ba1458e-6917-4a73-993d-333ce48a9145/eem212684-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3ba1458e-6917-4a73-993d-333ce48a9145/eem212684-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25546b91-774e-4b05-84b9-75af4ed7a2d8/eem212684-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3ba1458e-6917-4a73-993d-333ce48a9145/eem212684-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=eem212684-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Feem2.12684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of FHNCS//NVP@C full-cell. a) The model of a full battery. b) The CV curves at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) The charge/discharge curves at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of FHNCS and NVP@C in a half-cell system. d) The CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a voltage window from 0.01 to 3.8 V. e) The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) The cycling stability at current densities of 0.02, 0.07, 0.1, 0.3, 0.4, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>. g) The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at current densities of 0.02, 0.07, 0.1, 0.3, 0.4, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>. h) The long cycling stability at a current density of 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="eem212684-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="eem212684-sec-0003-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we synthesized a Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node spawned N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure (FHNCS) by using the modified MIL-88B as a template followed by epitaxial growth and sulfidation process. During the epitaxial growth process, the reduced Fe monomers catalyze the growth of N-doped carbon nanotubes to connect the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction node, forming a 3D scaffold structure. Wherein the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction is the core, and the N-doped carbon nanotube is the shell. Each component plays its own role. The N-doped carbon promotes the transfer of electrons between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS particles, and the tri-heterojunction facilitates the diffusion of electrons at the interface, to organize a 3D conductive net from outer to inner. The unique scaffold structure provides a multi-channel for the permeation of electrolytes, which offers more active sites and shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path. Meanwhile, inspired by the scaffold, the structure exhibits excellent mechanical stability to effectively alleviate the volume expansion during circulation. Furthermore, the Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe heterojunction can adjust the d-band center of Fe in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to enhance the interaction between Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S intermediate, which restrains the shuttle effect. Therefore, in a half-cell system, the FHNCS electrode demonstrates a high specific capacity of 436 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 84.7% and 73.4% of the initial capacity are maintained after 100 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 1000 cycles at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The FHNCS//NVP@C full cell exhibits an excellent initial reversible capacity of 158.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current rate of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and still 127 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained after 100 cycles, and 104.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved even at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. We believe this strategy gives an inspiration for constructing Fe-based anode of SIBs with a stable structure, which improves rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="eem212684-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="eem212684-sec-0004-title">4 Experiment Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0005">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0006">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0007">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0007-title"> Chemical reagents</h4>
                  
                  <p>The terephthalic acid (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, PTA, 99%), the citric acid monohydrate (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, GR, 99.8%), and the ferric chloride (FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, AR, 99.9%) were purchased from Macklin Limited Liability Company (LLC). The <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>NO, AR, 99.5%), dicyandiamide (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, DICY, 99.5%), and the sulfur powder (S, 99.5%) were purchased from Sigma LLC.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0008">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0008-title"> Preparation of the modified MIL-88B(Fe)</h4>
                  
                  <p>Quantities of 0.4983 g terephthalic acid and 0.05 g citric acid were dissolved in 15 mL <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide and stirred for 15 min to form a solution. Meanwhile, 0.5433 g ferric trichloride hexahydrate was dissolved in 15 mL of <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide, which was stirred for 15 min to form a solution. The above two solutions were mixed and continued to be stirred for 10 min, forming a brownish-yellow solution. Then, the obtained solution was transferred into a hydrothermal reactor. The reaction was carried out in an oven at 120 °C for 12 h. The modified MIL-88B(Fe) was achieved after centrifugation (4000 r/min, 10 min) and dried under vacuum. The fusiform MIL-88B(Fe) was obtained by the same procedure except for adding citric acid.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0009">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0009-title"> Preparation of the FHNCS</h4>
                  
                  <p>The modified MIL-88B(Fe) and dicyandiamide with a mass ratio of 1:10 were placed on both ends of the quartz boat and calcined in a tube furnace at 400 °C with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup> for 2 h under a nitrogen atmosphere. After that, the product was continued to calcine at 750 °C with a ramp-up rate of 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup> for 4 h in argon–hydrogen gas. The above procedure is the catalytic growth process. The product was taken out and placed on one end of the quartz boat, and the sulfur powder with a mass ratio of 1:4 was put into another end of the quartz boat. The quartz boat was coated by another one and calcined in a tube furnace at 350 °C with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> for 2 h in a nitrogen atmosphere to obtain the FHNCS. The products obtained with the same procedure except for the calcination temperature of 500, 650, and 700 °C during the catalytic growth process were identified as FHNCS-500, FHNCS-650, and FHNCS-700. The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/Fe/FeS tri-heterojunction aggregation spawned N-carbon nanotube scaffold structure (FANCS) was synthesized by the same procedure except for taking the fusiform MIL-88B(Fe) as a template. The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/FeS/C nanosheet (FCS) was prepared by the same procedure excepting adding the dicyandiamide. The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/FeS/C aggregated particle (FAP) was constructed without the citric acid modifying agent and dicyandiamide.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0010">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0010-title"> Material characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The X'Pert PRO MPD was applied to perform the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns. The secondary electronic resolution of the scanning electron microscope (SEM) image is 1.0 nm with a voltage of 15 kV, and the image of the transmission electron microscope (TEM) was tested by the Talos F200X. The instrument to test X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is the PHI 5000 VersaprobeIII (VP-III), which can provide high-performance micro-area spectrum and secondary electron imaging. The specific surface area and pore size distribution of the material can be measured using Mike ASAP 2460 instrument. The Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was recorded by the instrument with a model of NEXUS 470.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0011">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0011-title"> Electrochemical measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The half-cell (CR2032) was assembled in a glove box filled with inert gas directly using the FHNCS as the cathode (1.5–2 mg), the NaClO<sub>4</sub> as the electrolyte, the sodium metal and glass fiber membrane (Whatman GF/C) as anode and separator, respectively. The water and oxygen content were both controlled below 0.1 ppm. Different from the half-cell, the full-cell was assembled by taking the FHNCS as the anode and the NVP as the cathode. The galvanostatic current charge and discharge test was carried out using the battery test system (LANDCT 2001a). In a half-cell system, the voltage was set as 0.01–2.8 V (Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and the current densities were 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, 5.0, and 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In a full-cell system, the voltage window was 0.01–3.8 V and the current densities were 0.02, 0.07, 0.1, 0.3, 0.4, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The DH7000 electrochemical workstation was used to test cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0012">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0012-title"> The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient according to galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">
                     <div class="inline-equation" id="eem212684-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7dcb4725-d3ae-46a7-9134-d79d419749d7/eem212684-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:25750356:media:eem212684:eem212684-math-0002" display="block" overflow="scroll" location="graphic/eem212684-math-0002.png">
                              <mrow>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                    <mtext>GITT</mtext>
                                 </msup>
                                 <mo linebreak="goodbreak">=</mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mn>4</mn>
                                    <mi>πτ</mi>
                                 </mfrac>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                       <mfrac>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>m</mi>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>V</mi>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                          </mrow>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <msub>

                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mi>S</mi>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </mfrac>
                                    </mfenced>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>

                              </mrow></math>
                           </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>From this equation, the (diffusion coefficient) <i>D</i><sup>GITT</sup> of Na<sup>+</sup> can be calculated. Wherein the π, τ (the relaxation time), <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> (the molar volume of the electrode material), and <i>S</i> (the electrode/electrolyte contact area) are constant. The ∆<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> (a voltage change caused by a pulse) and ∆<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> (the voltage changes of galvanostatic current charge/discharge) can be found in the data.</div>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="eem212684-sec-0013">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="eem212684-sec-0013-title"> DFT calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>We have employed the first principles to perform all spin-polarization density functional theory (DFT) calculations within the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) using the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) formulation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0057_R_d109708465e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">57</a>-<a href="#eem212684-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0059_R_d109708465e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We have chosen the projected augmented wave (PAW) potentials to describe the ionic cores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0060_R_d109708465e1886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Phys. Rev. B">60</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0061_R_d109708465e1886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Phys. Rev. B">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taking the valence electrons into account, we used a plane wave basis and set it with a kinetic energy cutoff of 450 eV. Van der Waals interactions have been considered using the DFT-D3 method of Grimme et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#eem212684-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0062_R_d109708465e1891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Chem. Phys.">62</a>, <a href="#eem212684-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="eem212684-bib-0063_R_d109708465e1891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Comput. Chem.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronic energy was considered self-consistent when the energy change was smaller than 10<sup>−5</sup> eV. A geometry optimization was considered convergent when the energy change was smaller than 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. During the relaxation, the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and FeS adopted the Brillouin zone with 2 × 2 × 1 Gamma-centered grid, respectively. The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–FeS, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–Fe, and Fe–FeS adopted the Brillouin zone with 1 × 1 × 1 Gamma-centered grid, respectively. The 15 Å vacuum layer was normally added to the surface to eliminate the artificial interactions between periodic images. Spin polarized calculations were performed for this calculation.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
